                    }
                }
            }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A reference-grade wild soybean genome"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2019 10:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-03-14"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Efficient crop improvement depends on the application of accurate genetic information contained in diverse germplasm resources. Here we report a reference-grade genome of wild soybean accession W05, with a final assembled genome size of 1013.2&#8201;Mb and a contig N50 of 3.3&#8201;Mb. The analytical power of the W05 genome is demonstrated by several examples. First, we identify an inversion at the locus determining seed coat color during domestication. Second, a translocation event between chromosomes 11 and 13 of some genotypes is shown to interfere with the assignment of QTLs. Third, we find a region containing copy number variations of the Kunitz trypsin inhibitor (KTI) genes. Such findings illustrate the power of this assembly in the analysis of large structural variations in soybean germplasm collections. The wild soybean genome assembly has wide applications in comparative genomic and evolutionary studies, as well as in crop breeding and improvement programs. Wild relatives of crop plants are invaluable germplasm for genetic improvement. Here, Xie et al. report a reference-grade wild soybean genome and show that it can be used to identify structural variation and refine quantitative trait loci."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Efficient crop improvement depends on the application of accurate genetic information contained in diverse germplasm resources. Here we report a reference-grade genome of wild soybean accession W05, with a final assembled genome size of 1013.2âMb and a contig N50 of 3.3âMb. The analytical power of the W05 genome is demonstrated by several examples. First, we identify an inversion at the locus determining seed coat color during domestication. Second, a translocation event between chromosomes 11 and 13 of some genotypes is shown to interfere with the assignment of QTLs. Third, we find a region containing copy number variations of the <i>Kunitz trypsin inhibitor</i> (<i>KTI</i>) genes. Such findings illustrate the power of this assembly in the analysis of large structural variations in soybean germplasm collections. The wild soybean genome assembly has wide applications in comparative genomic and evolutionary studies, as well as in crop breeding and improvement programs.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Genomic information is the essential foundation of current crop improvement programs. Accurate information is required for effective tracking of genomic variations, mapping important quantitative trait loci (QTLs), and discovering novel alleles. These tasks are intrinsically dependent on the availability of a range of genetic resources and, most crucially, high-quality reference genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Morrell, P. L., Buckler, E. S. &amp; Ross-Ibarra, J. Crop genomics: advances and applications. Nat. Rev. Genet. 13, 85â96 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d14844e813">1</a></sup>. Wild germplasm contributes a significant proportion of the genetic resources of major crop species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Munoz, N., Liu, A., Kan, L., Li, M. W. &amp; Lam, H. M. Potential uses of wild germplasms of grain legumes for crop improvement. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 18, 328 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14844e817">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Zhang, H. Y., Mittal, N., Leamy, L. J., Barazani, O. &amp; Song, B. H. Back into the wild-apply untapped genetic diversity of wild relatives for crop improvement. Evol. Appl. 10, 5â24 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d14844e820">3</a></sup>. Although reference genomes are currently available for two soybean cultivars, the Williams 82 (Wm82) genome has been most commonly used for a range of applications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14844e824">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Shen, Y. et al. De novo assembly of a Chinese soybean genome. Sci. China Life Sci. 61, 871â884 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14844e827">5</a></sup>. However, these approaches alone have limitations and cannot fully address questions regarding large structural variations or complex genomic rearrangements. A high-quality reference genome from wild soybean is also a crucial tool for use in such studies, because it increases the precision of population genetic analysis of complicated genomes. For example, genomic information is the essential foundation for understanding domestication-related events that involved wild germplasms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim, M. Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14844e831">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045â1052 (2014)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14844e831_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Qi, X. P. et al. Identification of a novel salt tolerance gene in wild soybean by whole-genome sequencing. Nat. Commun. 5, 4340 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14844e834">8</a></sup>.</p><p>We have previously reported whole-genome sequencing data for wild and cultivated soybeans, and demonstrated the high genome diversity in wild soybean populations compared with cultivated soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Lam, H. M. et al. Resequencing of 31 wild and cultivated soybean genomes identifies patterns of genetic diversity and selection. Nat. Genet. 42, 1053âU1041 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14844e841">9</a></sup>. The genomic diversity of wild soybean has been confirmed and elaborated in reports by ourselves and others<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Kim, M. Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14844e845">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045â1052 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14844e848">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chung, W. H. et al. Population structure and domestication revealed by high-depth resequencing of Korean cultivated and wild soybean genomes. DNA Res. 21, 153â167 (2014)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14844e851">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Qiu, J. et al. Genome re-sequencing of semi-wild soybean reveals a complex Soja population structure and deep introgression. PLoS ONE 9, e108479 (2014)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14844e851_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Valliyodan, B. et al. Landscape of genomic diversity and trait discovery in soybean. Sci. Rep. 6, 23598 (2016)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14844e851_2">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Zhou, Z. K. et al. Resequencing 302 wild and cultivated accessions identifies genes related to domestication and improvement in soybean. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 408âU125 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14844e854">13</a></sup>. Despite several previous attempts at whole-genome assembly in wild soybeans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim, M. Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14844e858">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045â1052 (2014)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14844e858_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Qi, X. P. et al. Identification of a novel salt tolerance gene in wild soybean by whole-genome sequencing. Nat. Commun. 5, 4340 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14844e861">8</a></sup>, a high-quality reference genome has remained elusive. In resolution of this important issue, we report here a high-quality genome for the wild soybean accession W05. W05 has previously been employed to identify several agronomically important QTLs, together with the identification of the causal gene conferring salt tolerance in wild soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Qi, X. P. et al. Identification of a novel salt tolerance gene in wild soybean by whole-genome sequencing. Nat. Commun. 5, 4340 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14844e865">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Li, M. W. et al. QTLs regulating the contents of antioxidants, phenolics, and flavonoids in soybean seeds share a common genomic region. Front. Plant Sci. 7, 854 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14844e868">14</a></sup>. In this study, we not only demonstrate the power of W05 reference genome but also highlight its applicatibity in a wide range of comparative genomic and evolutionary studies, using a range of examples, such as the identification of large structural variations, QTLs, genes, and alleles. The advantages of combining high-quality reference genomes and optical mapping (OM) in studying structural variations among multiple accessions is also described.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">De novo sequencing and assembly</h3><p>State-of-the-art whole-genome sequencing technologies were used to assemble a high-quality reference genome for wild soybean accession W05 with long contigs and high sequence fidelity. PacBio subreads (85.5âGb) were error-corrected and de novo assembled into primary contigs (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">1a</a>). Sequences were then polished with PacBio subreads and Illumina paired-end reads (101.3âGb) (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">1b</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">1</a>). The polished contigs are 989.7âMb in length and are composed of 2281 sequences with a contig N50 (50% of the genome covered by contigs above this length) of 2.0âMb. Details for assembly procedures can be found in Methods section.</p><p>To anchor polished contigs onto chromosomes with high accuracy, two complementary technologies: OM (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">2</a>) and Hi-C sequencing (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">1</a>) were employed. Based on the optical contigs generated with the nickases Nt.BspQI and Nb.BssSI (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">1c</a>), two-enzyme hybrid scaffolding was performed to generate OM-sequence hybrid scaffolds (hybrid scaffolds) (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">1d</a>). The hybrid scaffolds comprised 1438 sequences with a total length of 1019.8âMb and a scaffold N50 of 13.9âMb (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">1d</a>). In addition, Hi-C contact frequency derived from Hi-C sequencing was used to order and orient the polished contigs into Hi-C scaffolds (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">1e</a>). The resulting Hi-C scaffolds comprised 1161 sequences, with a total length of 989.8âMb and a scaffold N50 of 48.5âMb (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">1e</a>). Superscaffolds were generated by merging hybrid scaffolds and Hi-C scaffolds (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">1f</a>).</p><p>After gap filling and polishing, the final assembly for W05 is 1013.2âMb in length, with 988.6âMb unambiguous bases (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">1g</a> and TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Tab1">1</a>). In total, 95.7% of sequences are anchored to 20 superscaffolds, corresponding to 20 chromosomes, whereas 43.6âMb in 1098 contigs remain unplaced. The contig N50 of the final assembly is 3.3âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Tab1">1</a>). The longest contig of the W05 final assembly is 23.2âMb in length, spanning 47.7% of chromosome 6. The contiguity of the W05 assembly is approximately a 17-fold improvement over the current reference genome Wm82_v2 and of similar quality as the recently published Chinese cultivated soybean reference genome of ZH13<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Shen, Y. et al. De novo assembly of a Chinese soybean genome. Sci. China Life Sci. 61, 871â884 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14844e933">5</a></sup> (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">3</a>). Contig N50, scaffold N50, and total assembled genome size of other soybean genome assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Kim, M. Y. et al. Whole-genome sequencing and intensive analysis of the undomesticated soybean (Glycine soja Sieb. and Zucc.) genome. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 22032â22037 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14844e941">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045â1052 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14844e944">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Qiu, J. et al. Genome re-sequencing of semi-wild soybean reveals a complex Soja population structure and deep introgression. PLoS ONE 9, e108479 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14844e947">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shimomura, M. et al. The Glycine max cv. Enrei genome for improvement of Japanese soybean cultivars. Int. J. Genom. 2015, 358127 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14844e950">15</a></sup> were compared (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">3</a>), but these genomes were not included in subsequent analysis, because they are highly fragmented.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 . Summary of W05 genome assembly and annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Assembly evaluation</h3><p>The completeness of this genome assembly was examined using the Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) evaluation score<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14844e1817">16</a></sup>. The completeness of W05 is comparable to the Wm82_v2 and ZH13 reference genomes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">4</a>). The mapping rate of the PacBio Isoform Sequencing (IsoSeq) full-length transcripts was 97.7% (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">5</a>). Cent91/92 soybean-specific centromeric repeats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Gill, N. et al. Molecular and chromosomal evidence for allopolyploidy in soybean. Plant Physiol. 151, 1167â1174 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14844e1827">17</a></sup> were found in 19 chromosomes, except chromosome 1 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig1">1e</a>). In addition, the <i>Arabidopsis</i>-type telomeric tandem repeat array<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Kaul, S. et al. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d14844e1838">18</a></sup> (CCCTAAA/TTTAGGG repeats) was found at both distal ends for 13 chromosomes and at a single distal end for the remaining 7 chromosomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig1">1e</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">6</a>). In contrast, only 9 and 7 chromosomes of Wm82_v2 and ZH13, respectively, contain telomeric tandem repeats at both distal ends. The completeness of telomeres in the W05 genome is therefore improved compared with that of Wm82_v2 and ZH13 (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">6</a>). GC content along each chromosome was calculated (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig1">1c</a>). Furthermore, using in silico PCR, 874 conserved, unique-site soybean simple sequence repeat markers were mapped to the W05 genome, which are evenly distributed on the 20 chromosomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig1">1d</a> and Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM5">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09142-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9142_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09142-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9142_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="883" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Distribution of W05 genomic features. The outer layer illustrates the 20 chromosomes of W05 in megabases (Mb). <b>a</b> Repeat coverage was calculated by the occupancy of repeat sequence in 1âMb window (step size: 500âKb). <b>b</b> Gene coverage was calculated by the occupancy of coding sequence in 1âMb window (step size: 500âKb). <b>c</b> GC content was calculated in a 200âKb window. <b>d</b> Position of simple sequence repeat (SSR) markers were indicated in purple. Marker information could be found in Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM5">1</a>. <b>e</b> Presence of telomeric tandem arrays and cent91/92 soybean specific type centromeric repeats were marked in pink and blue, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome annotation</h3><p>Protein-coding genes and alternative spliced isoforms were annotated by combining evidence generated from RNA-seq/PacBio IsoSeq transcript mapping, homology-based protein mapping, and ab initio prediction. In total, 234.7âGb of Illumina RNA-seq reads were collected from 31 samples at various development and physiological stages (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM6">2</a>). PacBio IsoSeq libraries were constructed in order to generate 414,750 full-length and non-chimeric transcripts (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">7</a>). In total, 89,477 protein-coding transcripts were annotated for 55,539 gene loci, with 69,455 transcripts (77.6%) having 5â²-untranslated region (UTR) and 71,271 transcripts (79.7%) having 3â²-UTR (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">8</a>). In addition, 82,567 transcripts (92.3%) encode proteins that contain predicted functional domains (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Tab1">1</a>). Features of the annotated transcripts in W05 are similar to those of Wm82_v2 and ZH13<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14844e1918">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Shen, Y. et al. De novo assembly of a Chinese soybean genome. Sci. China Life Sci. 61, 871â884 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14844e1921">5</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">8</a>). BUSCO evaluation shows that completeness of the W05 annotated gene set was comparable to that of Wm82_v2 and ZH13 (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">9</a>). A total of 288 microRNAs (miRNA), 1988 small nuclear RNAs (snRNA), and 147 ribosomal RNAs (rRNA) were identified in the wild soybean genome (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Tab1">1</a>). In addition, 892 transfer RNAs (tRNA) were identified, representing anti-codons for all 20 types of amino acids.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Identification and refinement of QTLs</h3><p>One major application of the wild soybean reference genome is the identification of QTLs, genes, and alleles. We previously constructed a recombinant inbred (RI) population by crossing W05 to a cultivated soybean Union (C08)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Qi, X. P. et al. Identification of a novel salt tolerance gene in wild soybean by whole-genome sequencing. Nat. Commun. 5, 4340 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14844e1946">8</a></sup>, which shared the same recurrent parent with Wm82. To demonstrate that W05 can be used effectively as a reference genome for QTL mapping, we make use of our previous published phenotypic data together with new data of seed size and sequencing reads of 96 core RI lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Qi, X. P. et al. Identification of a novel salt tolerance gene in wild soybean by whole-genome sequencing. Nat. Commun. 5, 4340 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14844e1950">8</a></sup> to construct binmaps, and map QTLs using W05 genome or Wm82_v2 genome as reference. The relative genomic location and span of QTLs are comparable when either genome was used as the reference, with very few discrepancies (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM7">3</a>). For example, the growth period QTL on chromosome 11 spans a 3.55âMb region in the Wm82_v2 genome, but only 500âKb in the W05 genome. This may be due to the low-quality assembly of Wm82_v2 in this region, as an ~3.8âMb sequence within this region, which was originally present in Wm82_v1, was not anchored to the chromosome in Wm82_v2 (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM7">3</a>).</p><p>The quality of the assembly down to the nucleotide level was assessed by examining known traits associated genes. For <i>Ncl</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Qi, X. P. et al. Identification of a novel salt tolerance gene in wild soybean by whole-genome sequencing. Nat. Commun. 5, 4340 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14844e1965">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Guan, R. X. et al. Salinity tolerance in soybean is modulated by natural variation in GmSALT3. Plant J. 80, 937â950 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14844e1968">19</a></sup>, <i>Rj2/Rfg1</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Yang, S. M., Tang, F., Gao, M. Q., Krishnan, H. B. &amp; Zhu, H. Y. R gene-controlled host specificity in the legume-rhizobia symbiosis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 18735â18740 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14844e1974">20</a></sup>, and <i>G</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Song, J. et al. Identification and validation of Loci Governing Seed Coat Color by Combining Association Mapping and Bulk Segregation Analysis in Soybean. PLoS ONE 11, e0159064 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14844e1980">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Wang, M. et al. Parallel selection on a dormancy gene during domestication of crops from multiple families. Nat. Genet. 50, 1435â1441 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14844e1983">22</a></sup> loci, the alleles in W05 match the observed phenotypes (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Tab2">2</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM7">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM8">4</a>). In addition, we have also identified known polymorphisms and additional alleles of the causal genes in the QTLs controlling growth period, flower color, seed coat color, and pubescence color in W05 (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Tab2">2</a>, Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM7">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM8">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Predicted phenotypes based on genomic assemblies and observed phenotypes<sup>*</sup></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To investigate a more complex case, we examined the <i>I</i> locus on chromosome 8, which determines the pigmentation of the seed coat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tuteja, J. H., Zabala, G., Varala, K., Hudson, M. &amp; Vodkin, L. O. Endogenous, tissue-specific short interfering RNAs silence the chalcone synthase gene family in Glycine max seed coats. Plant Cell 21, 3063â3077 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2424">23</a></sup>, a major trait that was selected during domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Song, J. et al. Identification and validation of Loci Governing Seed Coat Color by Combining Association Mapping and Bulk Segregation Analysis in Soybean. PLoS ONE 11, e0159064 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2428">21</a></sup>. It was reported that the dominant allele in cultivated soybeans contains an inverted repeat of the chalcone synthase (<i>CHS</i>) gene cluster, which triggers posttranscriptional gene silencing (PTGS) and inhibits the expression of <i>CHS</i> gene family members in the seed coat; hence, resulting in colorless seed coat and yellow seeds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Tuteja, J. H., Clough, S. J., Chan, W. C. &amp; Vodkin, L. O. Tissue-specific gene silencing mediated by a naturally occurring chalcone synthase gene cluster in Glycine max. Plant Cell 16, 819â835 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2439">24</a></sup>. Deletion that disrupts the inverted repeat <i>CHS</i> gene cluster in a revertant soybean accession resulted in seed coat color transition from colorless to pigmented<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tuteja, J. H., Zabala, G., Varala, K., Hudson, M. &amp; Vodkin, L. O. Endogenous, tissue-specific short interfering RNAs silence the chalcone synthase gene family in Glycine max seed coats. Plant Cell 21, 3063â3077 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2446">23</a></sup>.</p><p>W05 has a pigmented seed coat, whereas C08 has a colorless seed coat. A seed coat color QTL that overlaps with the known <i>I</i> locus was mapped (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM7">3</a>). The W05 reference genome possesses the same inverted repeat of the <i>CHS</i> gene cluster as Wm82 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig2">2a</a>), indicating that the inverted repeat is not sufficient to explain the seed coat color change during domestication.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09142-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9142_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09142-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9142_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="485" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Causal structural variation that controls soybean seed coat pigmentation. <b>a</b> Sequence comparison between W05 genome and Wm82 bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) sequences at the <i>I</i> locus region. <i>CHS</i> genes and subtilisin gene/gene fragments are indicated with blue and orange, respectively. <b>b</b> Top panel: cartoon shows the exon structure of the subtilisin gene fragment (orange), the <i>CHS1</i> gene (blue), and the Expressed Sequence Tag (EST) sequence Gm-c1069â6017. Positions of primers designed for PCR amplification of subtilisin-anti-<i>CHS1</i> chimeric transcript are indicated with black arrows. Bottom panel: PCR amplification of the subtilisin-anti-<i>CHS1</i> chimeric transcript. Experiment was repeated at least twice with independent samples. Marker: 1âKb Plus DNA ladder (NEB, cat. N3200S). NTC, no template control. <i>GmACT11</i> is used as a housekeeping control. Unprocessed gel image is provided in Source Data file. <b>c</b> Proposed model for the generation of siRNAs originated from a large structural rearrangement in the <i>I</i> locus. <i>CHS</i> genes and the subtilisin gene/gene fragments are illustrated as blue and orange, respectively. Arrowheads indicated the direction of transcription that causes the formation of double-stranded RNA. Cluster A and B are named according to a previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tuteja, J. H., Zabala, G., Varala, K., Hudson, M. &amp; Vodkin, L. O. Endogenous, tissue-specific short interfering RNAs silence the chalcone synthase gene family in Glycine max seed coats. Plant Cell 21, 3063â3077 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2510">23</a></sup>. <i>IR-CHS</i> gene cluster: inverted repeat of <i>CHS</i> gene cluster</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To identify the genetic variation that caused the seed coat color change during domestication, the W05 genome was compared with the two published bacterial artificial chromosomes of Wm82 that were previously used in <i>I</i> locus studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tuteja, J. H., Zabala, G., Varala, K., Hudson, M. &amp; Vodkin, L. O. Endogenous, tissue-specific short interfering RNAs silence the chalcone synthase gene family in Glycine max seed coats. Plant Cell 21, 3063â3077 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2535">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Tuteja, J. H. &amp; Vodkin, L. O. Structural features of the endogenous CHS silencing and target loci in the soybean genome. Crop Sci. 48, S49âS68 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2538">25</a></sup> (the <i>I</i> locus region is poorly assembled in Wm82_v2), as well as the recently published ZH13 genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Shen, Y. et al. De novo assembly of a Chinese soybean genome. Sci. China Life Sci. 61, 871â884 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2545">5</a></sup>. This comparative analysis reveals a complex structural rearrangement next to the <i>CHS</i> gene cluster in Wm82 and ZH13, which includes both inversion and gene duplication (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">2</a>). The inversion brought the promoter and the first four exons of a subtilisin gene (Glysoja.08G020214 in W05) to a position next to the <i>CHS</i> gene cluster in Wm82 and ZH13 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">2</a>). This finding indicates that the subtilisin promoter may drive the expression of a chimeric transcript that reads through the subtilisin gene fragment and anti-<i>CHS1</i> gene region to cause PTGS of the <i>CHS</i> genes. This is in agreement with the previous speculation based on Expressed Sequence Tag data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Clough, S. J. et al. Features of a 103-kb gene-rich region in soybean include an inverted perfect repeat cluster of CHS genes comprising the I locus. Genome 47, 819â831 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2575">26</a></sup>. To validate this hypothesis, the subtilisin-anti-<i>CHS1</i> chimeric transcript was successfully amplified with PCR primers specific to both the subtilisin gene fragment and the anti-<i>CHS1</i> genes using complementary DNA from developing seed coat of Wm82 in a strand-specific manner (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig2">2b</a>). The subtilisin-anti-<i>CHS1</i> chimeric transcript was able to form double-stranded RNA with the sense <i>CHS</i> mRNAs, hence causing PTGS to inhibit the expression of <i>CHS</i> genes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig2">2c</a>). Deletion of the <i>CHS</i> gene cluster B adjacent to the subtilisin gene fragment will disrupt PTGS and lead to the transition from a colorless seed coat to a pigmented seed coat, which is in agreement with the previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tuteja, J. H., Zabala, G., Varala, K., Hudson, M. &amp; Vodkin, L. O. Endogenous, tissue-specific short interfering RNAs silence the chalcone synthase gene family in Glycine max seed coats. Plant Cell 21, 3063â3077 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2604">23</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig2">2c</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Major structural changes compared with cultivated genomes</h3><p>Transposable elements (TEs) are repeated DNA sequences that make up of a significant proportion of plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Tian, Z. X. et al. Genome-wide characterization of nonreference transposons reveals evolutionary propensities of transposons in soybean. Plant Cell 24, 4422â4436 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2620">27</a></sup>. They are important source of variations for natural and artificial selection. Combining homology-based searches and de novo prediction, we identified 546.4âMb of repeat elements (53.9% of the total assembled W05 genome) (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Tab1">1</a>, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig1">1a</a>). The most abundant type of TEs in the W05 genome is the long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposon element class, comprising 34.1% of the genome. The predominant LTR type is LTR/gypsy family.</p><p>Through whole-genome sequence comparisons of W05 with Wm82_v2 and ZH13, we have identified ~2300â3000 TE insertions for each accession (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">10</a>). To identify Wm82- or ZH13-specific TE insertions, W05 was used as the reference genome. To identify W05-specific TE insertions, Wm82_v2 or ZH13 was used as the reference. In total, 361 and 350 W05 genes were found to contain TE insertions in Wm82_v2 and ZH13 genome, respectively. In contrast, 419 and 400 genes from Wm82_v2 and ZH13, respectively, were found to have TE insertions in W05 genome.</p><p>Gene Ontology (GO) enrichment analysis identified 29 and 8 enriched GO terms among the Wm82_v2 and ZH13 TE-affected genes, respectively. The enriched GO terms in both genomes mainly fall into the category of biological processes related to metabolism (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM9">5</a>). Both sets contain GO:0044238 (primary metabolic process) and GO:0043170 (macromolecule metabolic process). For W05-specific TE-affected genes, there are three and nine GO terms enriched relative to ZH13 and Wm82, respectively. Unlike the two cultivated soybean references, the enriched GO terms mainly fall into the category of binding-related molecular functions (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM9">5</a>) including GO:0036094 (small molecule binding), GO:0000166 (nucleotide binding), and GO:1901265 (nucleoside phosphate binding).</p><p>High-quality reference genomes also allow confident genome-wide detection of large structural variations, which cannot be achieved unambiguously solely by re-sequencing analysis. When comparing the genome sequence of W05 with Wm82_v2 and ZH13, good chromosome-to-chromosome collinearity relationships were found (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">3</a>). However, we also identified large structural variations (â&gt;100âKb inversions, intra-chromosomal translocations, and inter-chromosomal translocations) among the reference genomes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">11</a>). Compared with W05, there are 32 and 12 large structural variations in Wm82_v2 and ZH13, respectively (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">11</a>). Nine of these variations are shared between Wm82_v2 and ZH13.</p><p>The largest structural variation in W05 relative to Wm82 and ZH13 is the inter-chromosomal reciprocal translocation in W05 between chromosomes 11 and 13. Previously, this translocation was detected in some <i>Glycine soja</i> accessions by fluorescent in situ hybridization (FISH)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Findley, S. D. et al. A fluorescence in situ hybridization system for karyotyping soybean. Genetics 185, 727â744 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2664">28</a></sup>. Analysis of the assembled genomes reveals this translocation event in W05 in comparison with Wm82 and ZH13 (Supplementary FiguresÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">4</a>). The structure of W05 chromosomes 11 and 13 is supported by Hi-C contact frequency matrix data, which shows higher intra-chromosomal contact frequency than inter-chromosomal contact frequency (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">5</a>). Consistent translocation breakpoints were identified when comparing W05 with ZH13 or Wm82_v1 but not with Wm82_v2 (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">4</a>). The discrepancy is most likely to be due to mis-assembly of Wm82_v2 in this region. Sequence comparisons revealed that the translocation breakpoint is located around 34.38âMb on Chr11 and 27.06âMb on Chr13 in the W05 genome (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><p>We also found that the QTLs controlling trailing growth, seed number per plant, and pod number per plant are located within the translocated regions (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM7">3</a>). Therefore, the casual gene(s) are located on chromosome 13 in Wm82 and on chromosome 11 in W05 and some other soybeans (see below). If only Wm82 was used as the reference genome, these QTLs will be mis-placed to chromosome 13 in W05. Therefore, we have provided an important information for marker-assisted breeding or map-based cloning of the casual gene(s) for these QTLs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Reviewing large structural variations using OM</h3><p>Although comparisons of the W05 and other reference genomes allows identification of many structural variations, these variations could be accession-specific. Therefore, we expanded our scope to analyze more soybean accessions from diverse origins, including cultivated soybeans popularized in China, the United States, and Japan, as well as wild soybeans originating from China and Korea (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM10">6</a>). OM technology that can be used to study long-range genomic DNA up to 1âMb in length was employed together with an in silico map based on our high-quality W05 genome to detect structural variations in diverse soybean accessions. We can thus demonstrate that the W05 reference genome enhances genome comparisons at the kilobase scale. As proof-of-concept, several loci exhibiting different structural features among soybean accessions were investigated, including inversion in the <i>I</i> locus, the translocation between chromosomes 11 and 13, a previously reported region specific to cultivated soybean on chromosome 15<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045â1052 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2704">7</a></sup>, and gene copy number variation in a <i>Kunitz trypsin inhibitor</i> (<i>KTI</i>) gene cluster. Only soybean accessions with sufficient coverage and depth of optical contigs in the target regions were employed for comparative analysis (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM10">6</a>).</p><p>For the <i>I</i> locus, comparative analysis revealed that soybean accessions exhibiting a pigmented seed coat have no inversion (similar to W05), whereas those with colorless seed coats share the same inversion as Wm82 and ZH13 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig3">3a</a>). To further confirm the OM results, the inversion junction that creates the subtilisin-anti-<i>CHS1</i> chimeric transcript was amplified from genomic DNA of different soybean accessions by PCR. Consistent with the OM results, inversion junction could be amplified from all soybean accessions with colorless seed coats but not from those with pigmented seed coats (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09142-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9142_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09142-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9142_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="938" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Large structural variations in soybean genomes detected by OM. <b>a</b> Seed coat pigmentation causal inversion in the <i>I</i> locus. Pink regions are the aligned flanking regions of the <i>I</i> locus. Aligned blocks in the <i>I</i> locus are painted in different colors to illustrate the inversion and duplication in accessions with colorless seed coat. <b>b</b> Reciprocal inter-chromosomal translocation between chromosomes 11 and 13. Segments in blue and red are regions homologous to the W05 chromosomes 11 and 13, respectively. Segments in gray contains optical signals that cannot be aligned to the W05 in silico map. <b>c</b> A previously reported cultivated soybean-specific region on chromosome 15<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045â1052 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2762">7</a></sup>. Blue regions are the aligned flanking region of the previously proposed cultivated soybean-specific region. Segments that cannot be aligned with W05 in silico map are shown in gray. <b>d</b> Length polymorphism of a <i>KTI</i> gene cluster in chromosome 8. Orange triangles indicate the location of <i>KTI</i> genes in W05 (top track) and Wm82_v2 (bottom track), respectively. <i>KTI</i>, Kunitz trypsin inhibitor genes. Asterisks (*) next to the accession IDs indicate the use of in silico map instead of optical contigs</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using Wm82_v1, ZH13, and W05 as references, the translocation event between chromosomes 11 and 13 were also detected. One (W01) out of three (W02, K02) wild soybean accessions share the same chromosome topology with W05. In contrast, all cultivated soybean accessions shared the same topology with the Wm82_v1 and ZH13 in silico maps, regardless of their origin whether they were popularized in China, the United States, or Japan (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig3">3b</a>). Furthermore, the regions at the junction of the translocation are reasonably well conserved among different accessions, which suggests that the translocation was a single evolutionary event.</p><p>Previously, a region specific to cultivated soybean (Gm15 46.0â46.5âMb) was identified in a pan-genome study by mapping short reads of seven wild soybeans onto the Wm82_v1 genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045â1052 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2795">7</a></sup>. We revisited this region and compared the optical contigs of different soybean accessions. Interestingly, the targeted region appears to have diversified among the different cultivated accessions that we have tested from China, the United States, and Japan, whereas only Wm82 possesses such a large insertion (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig3">3c</a>). This previously reported <i>Glycine max</i>-specific region was confirmed as Wm82 specific.</p><p>Comparisons of multiple reference genomes, together with the OM data, allow identification of gene copy number variations. KTI are anti-nutritional factors and are hence not desirable traits for selection in breeding purposes. Marker-assisted breeding has been used to introduce a null allele of <i>KTI</i> from an exotic soybean germplasm into two commercialized lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Maranna S., et al. Introgression of null allele of Kunitz trypsin inhibitor through marker-assisted backcross breeding in soybean (Glycine max L. Merr.). BMC Genet. 17, 106 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2812">29</a></sup>. A <i>KTI</i> gene cluster was identified on chromosome 8 of W05 that contains 17 <i>KTI</i> genes. The optical signaling patterns in this region are similar for cultivated soybean accessions and diversify for wild soybean accessions. Wild soybean accessions W05, W02, and K01 possess long fragments, whereas W01 and all of the cultivated accessions tested (including Wm82) exhibited reduced size in this genomic region (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig3">3d</a>). In the annotated Wm82_v2 and ZH13 reference genomes, there are only 13 and 11 <i>KTI</i> genes, respectively, in this region (Supplementary FigureÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">7</a>), implying that copy number variations of <i>KTI</i> genes might have occurred through artificial selection.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>The availability of multiple high-quality reference genomes from diverse genetic backgrounds is a prerequisite for effective mining of crop genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yao, W., Li, G. W., Zhao, H., Wang, G. W., Lian, X. M. &amp; Xie, W. B. Exploring the rice dispensable genome using a metagenome-like assembly strategy. Genome Biol. 16, 187 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2844">30</a></sup>, especially in studies involving wild germplasm. The current Wm82_v2 reference genome alone cannot provide conclusive answers to questions regarding large structural variations and complex genomic rearrangement. A new version of Wm82 genome is under construction (<a href="http://www.soybase.org">www.soybase.org</a>), which will improve the quality over the current version. However, comparative genomic analysis using only the reference genome of Wm82 may still fail to uncover wild-specific genetic variations, as these may have been lost during domestication or artificial selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045â1052 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2855">7</a></sup>. Here we report the assembly of a high-quality reference genome of a wild soybean accession W05, which has many distinguishing features compared with the two existing reference genomes of cultivated soybeans (see Results).</p><p>Wild soybeans exhibit agronomic traits that are different to cultivated soybean (e.g., smaller seed size, higher pod number, and seed number per plants, etc.). We have identified QTLs related to key yield components. A seed size QTL was reported and the proper chromosomal locations of the QTLs regulating trailing growth, pod number per plant, and seed number per plant were assigned. These findings demonstrate the value of the W05 reference genome. The combination of this genomic information and a genetic population that includes wild soybeans will result in the step change required for future soybean breeding.</p><p>Several examples demonstrate the unique strength of the W05 reference genome. In partcular, an inversion associated with seed coat color was identified at the <i>I</i> locus by comparing W05 with Wm82 and ZH13 genomes. The PCR results and OM data presented here demonstrate that the inversion is the source of PTGS (FigsÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig2">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig3">3a</a>). Furthermore, major structural changes were identified between the W05 genome and those of two cultivated soybeans. One example is the reciprocal translocation between chromosomes 11 and 13 (Supplementary FiguresÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">4</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM1">11</a>). Our findings successfully identify the translocation breakpoints, refining previous results obtained using FISH technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Findley, S. D. et al. A fluorescence in situ hybridization system for karyotyping soybean. Genetics 185, 727â744 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2884">28</a></sup>. Together with the high base-to-base fidelity, the W05 genome provides an important tool for future investigations of the variations in genes and alleles in wild soybeans, which may not exist in modern cultivars due to domestication bottlenecks and poorly informed selection criteria<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lam, H. M. et al. Resequencing of 31 wild and cultivated soybean genomes identifies patterns of genetic diversity and selection. Nat. Genet. 42, 1053âU1041 (2010)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2888">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chung, W. H. et al. Population structure and domestication revealed by high-depth resequencing of Korean cultivated and wild soybean genomes. DNA Res. 21, 153â167 (2014)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2888_1">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Qiu, J. et al. Genome re-sequencing of semi-wild soybean reveals a complex Soja population structure and deep introgression. PLoS ONE 9, e108479 (2014)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2888_2">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Valliyodan, B. et al. Landscape of genomic diversity and trait discovery in soybean. Sci. Rep. 6, 23598 (2016)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2888_3">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Zhou, Z. K. et al. Resequencing 302 wild and cultivated accessions identifies genes related to domestication and improvement in soybean. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 408âU125 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2891">13</a></sup>.</p><p>TEs are major drivers of plant evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Tian, Z. X. et al. Genome-wide characterization of nonreference transposons reveals evolutionary propensities of transposons in soybean. Plant Cell 24, 4422â4436 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2898">27</a></sup>. They occupy more than half of the genome sequences of many crop species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Tian, Z. X. et al. Genome-wide characterization of nonreference transposons reveals evolutionary propensities of transposons in soybean. Plant Cell 24, 4422â4436 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2902">27</a></sup>. Accession-specific TE insertion events can be effectively identified by comparing de novo genome assembles, as repeated regions could cause ambiguities in re-sequencing approaches. Comparisons of the W05 genome with those of two cultivated soybeans revealed that genes with TE insertions that are found in the two cultivated soybean genomes are concentrated in metabolic pathways (Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#MOESM9">5</a>). Conversely, similar patterns were not observed in W05-specific TE-affected genes. As metabolic changes lead to variations that are important for soybean production and quality, we may speculate that TE-affected metabolic genes were unintentionally selected during domestication. However, more wild and domesticated germplasm should be tested to further explore this hypothesis. The highlighted importance of variations in metabolic genes during domestication is consistent with our previous observations showing that fixed single-nuclotide polymorphisms (SNPs) are concentrated in metabolic genes in the cultivated soybeans, compared with wild soybeans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Zhao, S. C. et al. Impacts of nucleotide fixation during soybean domestication and improvement. BMC Plant Biol. 15, 81 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2909">31</a></sup>.</p><p>The data presented here also demonstrate the value of the W05 reference genome and the optical contigs of other soybean accessions. Together, these results provide a powerful tool that can be used to uncover large structural variations in soybean germplasm collections (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig3">3</a>). We present evidence showing that the inversion found in the <i>I</i> locus (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig3">3a</a>) and the translocation between Chromosomes 11 and 13 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig3">3b</a>) are events that are found in multiple soybean genomes. Moreover, such comparisons reveal accession-specific genomic regions, e.g., a region on chromosome 15 that was previously reported as a feature of the cultivated soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045â1052 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2929">7</a></sup> is in fact unique to Wm82 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig3">3c</a>).</p><p>The strategy of mapping optical contigs to high-quality reference genomes will have a significant impact in soybean breeding programs. For example, new cultivars generated from radiation-driven mutations often contain major structural genomic changes and these structural variations could be effectively identified using this approach. This strategy also provides an elegant means to detect copy number variations, which are the basis of a number of phenotypic traits in humans, animals, and plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Dolatabadian, A., Patel, D. A., Edwards, D. &amp; Batley, J. Copy number variation and disease resistance in plants. Theor. Appl. Genet. 130, 2479â2490 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2940">32</a></sup>. We specifically investigated a region containing multiple trypsin inhibitor genes as an example of how the genome can be interrogated to identify genes that influence seed nutritional quality. Trypsin inhibitors are considered to have evolved as a protective measure against herbivores and bacterial infections. Trypsin inhibitor proteins account for 2â6% total protein of soybean seeds, which is the highest value found in a range of legumes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Vagadia, B. H., Vanga, S. K. &amp; Raghavan, V. Inactivation methods of soybean trypsin inhibitor - a review. Trends Food Sci. Tech. 64, 115â125 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14844e2944">33</a></sup>, reducing the nutritional value of the beans as food and/or animal feed. The genomic analysis reported here reveals that a genomic region on chromosome 8, which presumably contains a <i>KTI</i> gene cluster, has shrunk in cultivated soybeans from China and other countries compared with most of the wild soybeans from China and Korea (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09142-9#Fig3">3d</a>). This finding suggests that domestication may have involved selection for a reduced copy number of <i>KTI</i> genes.</p><p>In summary, this study provides information regarding the wild soybean that cannot be easily inferred from the reference genomes of the cultivated soybeans. As wild accessions are important genetic resources for crop improvement, the wild soybean genome reported here will be a valuable, if not indispensable, tool for use in a wide range of applications by legume researchers for comparative genomic and evolutionary studies, and soybean breeders for crop improvement programs.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Sample preparation and sequencing</h3><p>W05 is a Chinese wild soybean accession originally collected in Henan Province. Union (C08) is a cultivated soybean bred in the United States by crossing Williams and SL12, and then backcrossing five times with Williams. W05 and C08 was used to generate a RI population for the identification of important QTLs and the causal gene of a major QTL for salt tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Qi, X. P. et al. Identification of a novel salt tolerance gene in wild soybean by whole-genome sequencing. Nat.
